      XF IN B ENCH : B ENCHMARKING LLM S IN C OMPLEX
002   F INANCIAL P ROBLEM S OLVING AND R EASONING
003
004
005    Anonymous authors
006    Paper under double-blind review
007
008
009                                              A BSTRACT
010
011            Solving financial problems demands complex reasoning, multimodal data process-
012            ing, and a broad technical understanding, presenting unique challenges for current
013            large language models (LLMs). We introduce XF IN B ENCH, a novel benchmark
014            designed to evaluate LLM’s ability in solving compleX, knowledge-intensive
015            Financial problems across diverse graduate-level topics with multi-modal con-
016
               text. We identify five core capabilities of LLMs using XF IN B ENCH, i.e, termi-
               nology understanding, temporal reasoning, future forecasting, scenario planning,
017
               and numerical modelling. XF IN B ENCH features 4,235 examples derived from
018
               graduate-level finance textbooks, and consists of three tasks: Statement Judging,
019            Multi-choice Question Answering and Financial Calculation. Upon XF IN B ENCH,
020            we conduct extensive experiments on 18 leading models. The result shows that
021            o1 is the best-performing text-only model with an overall accuracy of 67.3%, but
022            still lags significantly behind human experts with 12.5%, especially in temporal
023            reasoning and scenario planning capabilities. We further construct a knowledge
024            bank with 3,032 finance terms for knowledge augmentation analysis, and find that
025            relevant knowledge to the question only brings consistent accuracy improvements
026            across five capabilities to small open-source model. Additionally, our error analy-
027
               sis reveals that rounding errors in middle of calculation and blindness to position
               and intersection of curves in the image are two primary issues leading to model’s
028
               poor performance in calculating and visual-context questions, respectively. These
029
               findings underscores the critical role XF IN B ENCH will play in the development of
030            general-purpose of AI agents of tackling complex, knowledge-intensive financial
031            problems with multi-modal context.
032
033
034   1   I NTRODUCTION
035
036   Finance constitutes a critical domain, characterized by the necessity for sophisticated problem-
037   solving skills. Beyond domain-specific knowledge, it necessitates advanced capabilities such as
038   temporal reasoning (Su et al., 2024; Wang & Zhao, 2024), future forecasting (Jin et al., 2024; Zhou
039
      et al., 2023b), scenario planning (Valmeekam et al., 2022; Geva et al., 2021), and numerical mod-
      eling (Zhao et al., 2024; Koncel-Kedziorski et al., 2024). Besides, complex finance problems in
040
      real world usually involves rich multimodal information, covering time series (Yu et al., 2023), long
041
      tabular (Reddy et al., 2024) and various charts (Masry et al., 2022; Lu et al., 2024). These complex-
042   ities present significant challenges for large language models (LLMs), thereby rendering finance an
043   appropriate testbed for the evaluation of LLMs.
044
045
      Numerous datasets have been curated to assess the reasoning abilities of AI systems in the finance
      domain, with most emphasizing quantity extraction and basic mathematical reasoning (see Table
046
      1). Existing datasets, including TAT-QA (Zhu et al., 2021), FinQA (Chen et al., 2021), MultiHiertt
047
      (Zhao et al., 2022), PACIFIC (Deng et al., 2022) and ConvFinQA (Chen et al., 2022), primarily focus
048   on quantity extraction and basic numerical calculations using company’s financial reports. However,
049   they lack questions that entail extensive financial knowledge or complex reasoning processes. More
050   recently, some benchmarks have been introduced to assess the performance of LLMs on knowledge-
051   intensive finance tasks. For instance, BizBench (Koncel-Kedziorski et al., 2024) collects past finance
052   datasets for quantity extraction and knowledge examination to test LLMs’ business and financial un-
053   derstanding; KnowledgeFMATH (Zhao et al., 2024) emphasize LLMs’ mathematical reasoning and
      code completion abilities within the finance domain; and FinEval (Zhang et al., 2023) focuses on

071   Figure 1: Accuracies of leading LLMs and human performance on XF IN B ENCH across (a) five
072   capabilities for complex finance problem solving and (b) mathematical reasoning types(Lu et al.,
073   2024). Accuracies for o1 and Llama-3.1-405B here do not include questions with visual context.
074
075
076   understanding finance concepts in Chinese. Nevertheless, these benchmarks still do not address
077   the advanced capabilities necessary for solving complex financial problems like temporal reason-
078   ing, forecasting, and planning. To bridge this gap, we introduce XF IN B ENCH, a novel benchmark
079   specifically designed to evaluate LLM’s ability in solving complex, knowledge-intensive financial
080   problems across diverse graduate-level topics with multi-modal context.
081   XF IN B ENCH consists of 4,235 examples derived from graduate-level finance textbooks that en-
082   sures the complexity of financial problems in our dataset, and brings convenience to annotation of
083   ground-truth knowledge to each problem. Different from existing datasets that only evaluate the
084   model’s grasp of specialized financial vocabulary, i.e, Terminology Understanding, XF IN B ENCH
085   identifies four more advanced capabilities essential for complex finance problem-solving: (1) Tem-
086   poral Reasoning, involving the comprehension of time-based data and temporal relationships; (2)
087
      Future Forecasting, testing logical reasoning in predicting financial trends based on theoretical fi-
      nance models; (3) Scenario Planning, analyzing different potential future scenarios to assess their
088
      impact on financial decisions and strategies; and (4) Numerical Modelling, which involves con-
089
      structing structured representations of companies and products’ financial performance. Moreover,
090   XF IN B ENCH includes three tasks: statement judging, which evaluates the model’s understanding
091   of finance concepts; multi-choice question answering, which assesses strategic decision-making and
092   predictive capabilities with visual data; and financial calculation, which tests mathematical reason-
093   ing in finance. To further investigate how domain-specific knowledge could boost LLM’s perfor-
094   mance on our complex financial problems, we also design a knowledge bank with 3,032 finance
095   terms and ask human experts to annotate the ground-truth knowledge to each problem. Detailed
096   capability definitions can be found in §A, and data collection pipeline in §2 and §A.
097   We conduct extensive experiments on XF IN B ENCH to evaluate the complex finance problem-
098   solving ability of 18 leading LLMs , along with knowledge augmentation analysis and error analysis.
099   Our models include nine close-source models (e.g, o1, gpt-4o, claude-3.5-sonnet, etc.), two multi-
100   modal open-source models (i.e, Llama-3.2-Vision 11B and 90B), and seven text-only open-source
101   models (e.g, Llama-3.1, Mixtral 8×7B). We implement the Chain-of-Thought (CoT) prompting
102   method for all three tasks, and additionally apply Program-of-Thought (PoT) for financial calcula-
103   tion. Moreover, we establish a human performance baseline of human experts with finance degree.
104
      We show that XF IN B ENCH, featuring graduate-level topics and advanced capabilities for complex
      finance problem-solving, is a challenging benchmark with human performance reaching only 79.8%.
105
106   Our results indicate that o1 is the best-performing text-only model with an overall accuracy of
107   67.3%, while claude-3.5-sonnet achieves the highest accuracy of 64.0% when visual-context ques-
      tions included (§3.2). Despite that LLMs achieve comparable performance with human in termi-

121
      nology understanding, as shown in Figure 1, they still significantly lag behind human experts in
122   more advanced capabilities for complex finance problem-solving, including temporal reasoning
123   and scenario planning—especially when visual context is involved. Even if we augment models
124   with ground-truth knowledge from knowledge bank, the improvements across advanced capabili-
125   ties are still limited and inconsistent, except for small open-source model (§3.3). Moreover, our
126   error analysis reveals that rounding error in the intermediate steps of calculation and model’s blind-
127   ness of position and intersection of curves in the image (Rahmanzadehgervi et al., 2024) are two
128   inescapable issues leading to the poor performance in calculating and visual-context questions, re-
129   spectively (§3.4). These findings highlight that XF IN B ENCH represents a rigorous and challenging
130
      benchmark, offering a critical tool for advancing the development of LLMs in complex financial
      problem-solving and reasoning.
131
132
133   2     DATASET C ONSTRUCTION
134
135   Our benchmark, XF IN B ENCH, is developed to support complex reasoning in knowledge-intensive
136   finance tasks. We began by collecting questions and answers from three graduate-level finance text-
137   books and their solution manuals, while also building a knowledge bank of finance terms. Human ex-
138   perts annotated each question-answer pair with relevant finance terms to enrich the dataset. However,
139   since open-ended and calculation-based questions pose challenges for LLM evaluation, we lever-
140   aged GPT-4o to further annotate and expand the dataset, enhancing both its size and suitability for
141   LLM assessments. Lastly, we conducted a rigorous quality validation process with human experts
142
      to ensure the dataset’s accuracy and relevance. The final XF IN B ENCH dataset encompasses three
      key tasks—statement judging, multi-choice question answering, and financial calculation—and is
143
      supplemented by a comprehensive knowledge bank of finance terms and definitions.
144
145
146   2.1   I NITIAL DATA C OLLECTION
147
      Collection of Initial QA datasets. To ensure the complex and knowledge-intensive properties of
148
      our benchmark, we extract after-class questions from three classic graduate-level finance textbooks
149
      that cover most finance topics, i.e. Fundamentals of Corporate Finance, Options Futures and Other
150   Derivative, and The Economics of Money Banking and Financial Markets. We also download their
151   solution manuals from official websites to collect the gold answers to their after-class questions. We
152   then leverage OCR techniques via pdfplumber library to extract the text from PDF of textbooks
153   and solution manuals. We extract the questions and answers at the end of each chapter, and take
154   screenshots of tables and figures in context if any. In total, we collect 2,018 after-class questions
155   from textbooks, 343 of them with visual or tabular context. Tabular context saved in images are
156   processed by GPT-4o-mini to be stored in L ATEX format.
157   Classifying QA into Tasks. We classify after-class questions collected from textbooks into three
158   tasks: statement judging, multi-choice question answering, and financial calculation. Questions
159   that evaluate the basic understanding of finance concepts and theoretical models are classified into
160   statement judging task. Questions that focus on the application of financial strategies and models are
161   classified into multi-choice question answering task. Some questions may be classified into both two
      tasks. For questions that involve numerical reasoning, we classify them into financial calculation

186
187
188   task. Finally, 813 questions belong to the statement judging task, 624 to the multi-choice question
189   answering task, and 858 to the financial calculation task.
190
      Collection of Knowledge Bank. We construct a knowledge bank that consists of finance terms with
191
      definition for knowledge augmentation analysis during evaluation. We use the subject index at the
192   end of each textbook to acquire the finance terms and their corresponding page ranges in textbook.
193   We then manually extract the definition of each term from the corresponding pages. It is worth
194   noting that some terms may share the same pages, indicating that they share the same definition.
195   In total, we collect 3,032 terms with 1,766 unique definitions. Further details of knowledge bank
196   construction can be found in §B.3.
197
      Bridging QA and Knowledge Bank. We so far have collected after-class question-answer pairs
198   and finance terms in each textbook, which are initially linked through chapters. In each chapter,
199   a collection of finance terms is introduced in the main body, followed by after-class questions in
200   the end. Human experts are then instructed to annotate each after-class question with 1-to-3 most
201   relevant finance terms from the main body of the same chapter. Finally, a question is annotated with
202   1.3 terms on average. Further details of human annotation can be found in §B.3.
203
204   2.2     GPT-4 O E NHANCED A NNOTATION
205
206   After-class questions from textbooks are mostly open-ended or consisting of a series of sub-
207   questions, making it difficult to evaluate the model’s response. For instance, the answer to the
208   open-ended question “Discuss the advantages and disadvantages of options and forward contracts“
209   includes a list of properties of options and future contracts; the calculation question “An investment
210   offers ... If the payment occurs for 15 years, what is its value? For 40 years? Forever?“ contains a
211   series of sub-questions with different final answers. To ensure each question in XF IN B ENCH having
212
      a clear final answer to be evaluated accurately and conveniently, we leverage GPT-4o to process
      these questions under a Generate-then-verify framework (Zhang et al., 2024).
213
214   We first use few-shot prompts to ask GPT-4o to transform open-ended questions into those with clear
215   final answers. For statement judging task, we ask GPT-4o to extract both true and false statements
      from each after-class question (see Appendix G.2.1). To ensure a balanced representation of true and


                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      4
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


216
      false statements, we apply two prompt templates with the same after-class questions as few shots,
217   but one with true statements and one with false statements. For multi-choice question answering
218   task, we follow STARC rules (Berzak et al., 2020) to ask GPT-4o to first extract a clear and complete
219   question from the after-class question, and then create three candidate choices given the gold answer
220   (see Appendix G.2.2). Among these choices, one is the correct answer with evidence, and the other
221   two are misleading choices that either shows a misunderstanding of the gold answer or is made up
222   by GPT-4o itself. For financial calculation task, we ask GPT-4o to split the after-class question into
223   a series of independent questions with clear final answers (see Appendix G.2.3). In this stage, 6,227
224   questions are generated from after-class questions.
225   We then leverage GPT-4o to verify the quality of questions in the generation stage from multiple
226   dimensions. We primarily evaluate Correctness and Completeness of the generated question and
227   answer. Specifically, we evaluate whether (1) the question provides the complete background infor-
228   mation to get its final answer, and (2) the final answer is correct to the question given the after-class
229   question and its gold answer. Furthermore, to ensure the independence of questions in statement
230   judging task, we verify if, within the same after-class question, true statements provide no evidence
231
      to support that false statement(s) is wrong. For multi-choice question answering task, we verify if
      the two misleading choices are exclusive to, but share the similar wording and length with the cor-
232
      rect choice. For financial calculation task, we verify if the final answers are numerical without any
233
      text included. Finally, 35.2% questions are discarded in the verification stage. Details of automatic
234   annotation can be found in §B.2.
235
236
      2.3   DATA Q UALITY VALIDATION
237
238   We conduct a comprehensive validation protocol to ensure the high quality of the annotated data.
239   For each annotated question, we assign three evaluators to validate whether: 1) the question is fluent
240   and contains complete information to get the final answer; 2) the final answer is correct according to
241   the gold answer of after-class question; 3) the annotated finance terms are helpful for answering the
242   question. We ask the evaluators to rate all examples in XF IN B ENCH on a scale of 1 to 5 individually.
243   We then calculate the proportions of examples with average score S ≥ 4: question fluency 97.1%,
244   question completeness 96.8%, answer correctness 98.0%, knowledge helpfulness 91.2%. The high
245
      scores illustrate the high quality of XF IN B ENCH. Further details can be found in §C.1.
246
247   2.4   DATA S TATISTICS
248
      The main statistics of XF IN B ENCH are presented in Table 2. XF IN B ENCH consists of 4,235 exam-
249
      ples, divided into two subsets: validation and test. The division is based on random sampling over
250
      the after-class questions from textbooks. validation contains 1,000 examples, intended for model
251   development validation or for those with limited computing resources. The test set features the
252   remaining 3,235 examples for standard evaluation. Notably, the answer labels for test will not be
253   publicly released to prevent data contamination, and we will maintain an online evaluation platform.
254   Additionally, the knowledge bank consists of 3,032 finance terms with 1,766 unique definitions.
255   There are 28 finance concepts in our benchmark, exceeding most existing datasets (see Figure 3).
256   Detailed statistics of XF IN B ENCH and knowledge bank can be found in §C.
257
258
      3     E XPERIMENTS
259
260
      We conduct qualitative and quantitative studies to provide a comprehensive evaluation of leading
261   LLMs for complex reasoning in knowledge-intensive finance tasks using XF IN B ENCH.
262
263
      3.1   E XPERIMENTAL S ETUP
264
265   We evaluate the models on the test set of XF IN B ENCH uder two setups: 1) Multimodal Large Lan-
266   guage Models (MLLMs) who allow visual input, including gpt-4o (OpenAI, 2024b), gpt-4o-mini
267   (OpenAI, 2024a), claude-3.5-sonnet (Anthropic, 2024a), claude-3-opus, claude-3-haiku (Anthropic,
268   2024b), gemini-1.5-flash and gemini-1.5 pro (Team, 2024b), and Llama-3.2-Vision models (Meta,
269   2024b), and 2) Text-only Large Language Models who only allow textual input, including o1 (Ope-
      nAI, 2024d), o1-mini (OpenAI, 2024c), deepseek-chat (DeepSeek-AI, 2024), Llama-3.1 models

289
      (Meta, 2024a), Llama-3 models (Meta, 2024c), and Mixtral-7×8B (Jiang et al., 2024). All MLLMs
290
      allow text-only input except for Llama-3.2-Vision models, which we feed with a blank image in text-
291
      only tasks. Additionally, we evaluate the above models on the validation set of KnowledgeFMATH
292   (Zhao et al., 2024) (200 examples), and a random sample of the test set of BizBench (Koncel-
293   Kedziorski et al., 2024) (500 examples), for more comprehensive analysis and broader coverage of
294   financial tasks. More details can be found in D.1.
295
      We apply Chain-of-Thought (CoT) reasoning method (Wei et al., 2022) and use Accuracy as evalua-
296
      tion metrics in statement judging and multi-choice question answering tasks. In financial calculation
297
      task, we apply Program-of-Thought (PoT) method (Chen et al., 2023) in addition and use two eval-
298   uation metrics: 1) Accuracy of exact matching with correct answers, i.e. AccEM , and 2) Accuracy
299   allowing the model’s answer to be within 0.5% of the correct answer, i.e. AccERR .
300
301
      We further conduct knowledge augmentation analysis that allows access to external knowledge base
      during evaluation. We investigate 1) BM25 as sparse retriever and 2) OpenAI Ada embedding (Ope-
302
      nAI, 2022) as dense retriever to retrieve the top-n question-relevant finance terms from knowledge
303
      bank, where n is set to be 3. Further details of experiment setup can be found in §D.
304
305
306   3.2    M AIN R ESULTS
307
308   We compare the performance of 18 leading models, including MLLMs and text-only LLMs, on
309
      XF IN B ENCH, BizBench and KnowledgeFMATH in Table 3. We also establish a human perfor-
      mance baseline with three graduate-level human experts in Finance over a random sample from test
310
      set with 1,000 examples. Further details can be found in §D.3.
311
312   Among MLLMs, claude-3.5-sonnet achieves the best performance with 64.1% accuracy on XF IN -
313   B ENCH, followed by gpt-4o with 63.6% accuracy who achieve the highest accuracy in visual-context
314   questions, i.e., 65.3%. On the text-only LLM side, o1 achieves the highest accuracy in almost all
315
      tasks of XF IN B ENCH, with 67.3% overall accuracy; however, it still falls 12.5% short of human
      performance, highlighting that there is a significant scope for further improvements on our bench-
316
      mark. Open-source models with large parameter size, i.e, Llama-3.1-405B, achieves comparable
317
      performance with o1-mini and even outperforms gpt-4o-mini in text-only tasks on XF IN B ENCH.
318   However, most open-source models achieve underwhelming performance, attributed to their lack of
319   domain knowledge and mathematical reasoning ability. Additionally, enhanced performance on the
320   quantity extraction task of BizBench and the financial calculation task of KnowledgeFMATH high-
321   lights XF IN B ENCH as a more sophisticated and challenging benchmark within the finance domain.
322   AccERR scores in BizBench and KnowledgeFMATH are significantly higher than those in XF IN -
323   B ENCH for most models in calculating tasks. The model rankings across the three benchmarks are
      largely consistent, as indicated by the distribution of red cells in Table 3.

355
356   We observe that the PoT prompting method deteriorates the performance of most models in fi-
357   nancial calculation task. To better analyze the reasons for these differing performance outcomes,
358   we examine the execution rate of models under PoT prompting on XF IN B ENCH, measuring how
359   many of the generated Python programs are executable (Zhao et al., 2024). Figure 5(b) illustrates
360
      the relationship between execution rate and accuracy AccERR across different models, indicating
      that the degraded performance when applying PoT prompting is attributable to the low execution
361
      rate. For instance, while Llama-3.1-405B achieves competitive performance using CoT prompting,
362
      it struggles to consistently generate executable Python solutions, leading to lower accuracy with
363   PoT prompting. Interestingly, while o1’s execution rate lags behind most close-source models, it
364   achieves the highest accuracy score on AccERR , witnessing its strong and efficient reasoning ability
365   over complex tasks. We further report fine-grained results during evaluation in §E.
366
367   3.3   K NOWLEDGE AUGMENTATION M ETHOD
368
369   We evaluate the performance of models augmented with external knowledge base, and apply two
370   types of retrievers to acquire the relevant knowledge term to the question, i.e. BM25 and Ada Embed.
371   Recalling that we have annotated the most relevant finance terms for each question, we design a
372   Oracle setting, where models are provided with the ground-truth finance term(s) of each question.
373   We report the accuracy improvements of four models when augmented with a knowledge bank in
374   Figure 4. For various retrieving settings, we find that the Oracle setting leads to the most robust
375   improvements on most models, highlighting the high quality of our annotated dataset. Models em-
376   ploying a dense retriever based on Ada embedding achieve higher accuracy improvements compared
377   to those using a sparse retriever with BM25, for most models. Furthermore, we report the accuracy
      improvements across five financial capabilities under Oracle setting in Figure 4(b). While the im-

389
      overall accuracy changes in different retrieving settings. (b) illustrates the accuracy changes across
390   five capabilities for complex finance problem solving in Oracle setting.

392
393   provements in terminology understanding are consistently positive across all models, ground-truth
394   knowledge augmentation does not always lead to better performance in advanced capabilities. The
395   improvements across four advanced capabilities are inconsistent for most models, even experienc-
396   ing negative changes, while the smallest open-source model, i.e, Llama-3.1-8B, have all positive
397   improvements across all capabilities. Details of knowledge augmentation can be found in §E.

399   3.4     E RROR A NALYSIS
400
      We conduct error analysis on two tasks, i.e. the financial calculation task and the visual-context
401
      multiple-choice question answering task, and knowledge augmentation method. For analysis on two
402
      tasks, we randomly select 400 and 100 samples from responses of best performers in each task, i.e.,
403   o1 and gpt-4o. For analysis on knowledge augmentation method, we randomly select 100 samples
404   from responses of gpt-4o that deliver wrong final answers under Oracle setting. Human annotators
405   are then instructed to label various error types among these responses. Details can be found in §F.1.
406
      Error Analysis of Financial Calculation. Based on our observation, two primary reasons of incor-
407
      rect responses in calculating task are: 1) Rounding Error that exists in the intermediate calculating
408
      steps, and 2) Knowledge Misuse if applying wrong or incomplete finance formulas for calculation.
409   Annotators are instructed to decide whether the reasoning path is correct and whether any error type
410   exists in o1’s responses. As illustrated in Figure 5(a), 55.2% of o1’s response had correct reasoning
411   path without intermediate rounding error or knowledge misuse - but might contain rounding error in
412   the final step. Knowledge misuse appears more frequently in incorrect-reasoning responses, while
413   rounding error often exists in correct reasoning process. For better illustration, we display an ex-
414   ample of o1’s response containing both two errors in Figure 6(b). In this example, o1 fails to use
415   the primary property of American options, i.e. exercising the option before expiration date for profit
416   maximization, and hence leads to unnecessary calculation in the following nodes. It also presents
417
      a rounding error when building binomial tree, which inevitably leads to an incorrect answer in the
      end. Additionally, we present a case of how knowledge augmentation could help improve gpt-4o’s
418
      complex reasoning ability in finance task in Figure 6(a). The gold formula prompts to incorporate
419
      temporal and statistical reasoning abilities for calculation of future value.
420
421   Error Analysis of Visual Context. The error types identified in the visual-context multiple-choice
422   question answering task are as follows: 1) Blindness (Rahmanzadehgervi et al., 2024), where the
423
      model struggles with identifying the position and/or intersection of two curves, and 2) Knowledge
      Misuse, occurring when irrelevant knowledge is introduced, thereby disrupting the reasoning path.
424
      Annotators are first instructed to determine if the explanation in the model’s response is correct,
425
      partially correct, or wrong (Lu et al., 2024), considering both image description and reasoning pro-
426   cess. For partially correct and wrong explanations, annotators are then asked to identify the two
427   error types among gpt-4o’s responses. As illustrated in Figure 7(a), 11.5% of its response had incor-
428   rect answer with incorrect explanations. Interestingly, we observe that gpt-4o responds with partial
429   (9.6%) or completely (11.5%) incorrect explanations despite giving the correct answer. In Figure
430   7(b), we find that the model responds with correct reasoning but either blindness (49%) or knowl-
431   edge misuse (6.1%), leaving an overall impression of being a wrong explanation. It is worth noting
      that 71.4% of its responses contain blindness in correct reasoning path, highlighting that blindness

      is a major source of errors in the generative foundation models (Rahmanzadehgervi et al., 2024).
472
      We present two examples of gpt-4o’s responses to illustrate the two error types. In Figure 9(a), gpt-
473
      4o correctly identifies the temporal trend in the image, interpret its economic implication, and then
474   analyze its effect on goods price. Correct image description and reasoning path in finance domain
475   leads to the correct final answer. By contrast, in Figure 9(b), while gpt-4o outputs the correct final
476   answer, its response contain both two error types, i.e., misunderstanding of supply in bond market
477   and blindness to the intersection of Rd2 and Rs curves. Overall, our analysis of gpt-4o highlights
478   its modes of failure, which could guide future foundation model design to address these issues.
479
      Error Analysis of Knowledge Augmentation. We identify three error types when models are
480   augmented with ground-truth finance term(s) but still fail to deliver the correct final answers: 1)
481   Reasoning Error that appears in the model’s reasoning process and has no direct relation to the aug-
482   mented knowledge; 2) Over Thinking, in which case augmented knowledge provides direct solutions
483   but the model reasons further steps that go out of the question’s scope; 3) Over Reliance, in which
484   case the model’s reasoning process is entirely guided by augmented knowledge, foregoing simpler
485   approaches to answering the question. As illustrated in Figure 8, most of wrong final answers for
      calculating questions, especially those requiring temporal reasoning and numerical modelling ca-

      Figure 8: Error analysis of GPT-4o in knowledge augmentation: (a) presents proportions of each
512
      error type across three tasks and in total; (b) presents proportions across five finance capabilities.
513
514
515   pabilities, are caused by reasoning error that has little to do with augmented knowledge, such as
516   rounding error. Over thinking is most frequently observed in multiple-choice questions requiring
517   future forecasting capability, suggesting that GPT-4o exhibits a tendency to engage in deeper rea-
518   soning when addressing questions involving predictions of future events. Moreover, over reliance is
519   most commonly encountered in questions requiring scenario planning capability, which emphasizes
520   the model’s ability to plan rather than strictly adhering to the instructions provided in the augmented
521   knowledge. Detailed guidelines and cases studies of error analysis can be found in F.
522   Among the error types discussed above, blindness imposes greater demands on the visual-textual
523   alignment capabilities of models. This limitation is likely attributable to the late-fusion approach
524   (Alayrac et al., 2022; Liu et al., 2023) used for integrating vision into LLMs, suggesting that an
525   early-fusion strategy (Team, 2024a; Tong et al., 2024) may offer a more effective solution. Errors
526   such as rounding errors, knowledge misuse, and knowledge-augmentation errors could potentially
527   be alleviated through more advanced prompting techniques, such as self-consistency CoT (Wang
528   et al., 2023), least-to-most CoT (Zhou et al., 2023a), etc.
529
530   4   C ONCLUSION
531
532
      In this work, we introduced XF IN B ENCH, a novel benchmark designed to evaluate LLM’s ability in
533
      solving complex, knowledge-intensive financial problems across diverse graduate-level topics with
534   multi-modal context. We identified five core capabilities of LLMs using XF IN B ENCH, i.e, termi-
535   nology understanding, temporal reasoning, future forecasting, scenario planning, and numerical
536   modelling. Upon XF IN B ENCH, we conducted extensive experiments on 18 leading models. The
537   result shows that o1 is the best-performing text-only model with an overall accuracy of 67.3%, but
538   still lags significantly behind human experts with 12.5%. We further constructed a knowledge bank
539   with 3,032 finance terms for knowledge augmentation method and conduct detailed error analysis
      across different tasks and models.

      A     DATA C OLLECTION G UIDELINES

      A.1    F INANCIAL AND M ATHEMATICAL C APABILITY D EFINITION
867
868   We define five core capabilities required for tackling complex finance problems in Table 4, along
869   with their proportions. We also introduce five mathematical reasoning types from M ATHE V ISTA
870   (Lu et al., 2024): arithmetic reasoning, statistical reasoning, algebraic reasoning, numeric com-
871   monsense, and logical reasoning. For annotation of questions in our financial calculation task with
872   these mathematical reasoning types, we use a zero-shot prompt with definitions of these reasoning
873   types, and leverage gpt-4o-mini to label each question with 1-to-2 mathematical reasoning type(s).
874   See Table 5 for annotation results. The prompt template for annotation can be found in Appendix
875
      G.2.4.
876
877   Table 4: Definitions of five capabilities of solving complex, knowledge-intensive finance problem.
878
                Capability       Description
879
                                 It refers to the model’s ability to accurately understand finance concepts, including
880    Terminology Understanding
                                 standard financial terms, acronyms, accounting principles, various financial instru-
881
                (56.1%)
                                 ments, regulatory terminologies, and economic indicators.
882                              It focuses on understanding temporal relations in time-based data, and making time-
          Temporal Reasoning
883                              sensitive decisions. It often involves data over various time periods, like quarterly
                (21.7%)
                                 earnings reports, historical stock performance and future cash flow projections.
884
                                 It involves predicting future values or trends of financial indicators such as output
885        Future Forecasting
                                 level, price level and inflation rates. It requires the model to use economic theories
                 (5.0%)
886                              and quantitative methods to generate forecasts for strategic decision-making.
887                              It is the process of generating and analyzing different possible future scenarios to as-
           Scenario Planning
888                              sess their impact on financial decisions and strategies. It requires considering various
                 (7.6%)
                                 uncertainties and variables to prepare for various outcomes.
889
                                 It involves creating structured representations of a company or product’s financial per-
890       Numerical Modelling
                                 formance. Related questions typically include financial statements like income state-
                (17.2%)
891                              ments, balance sheets, and cash flow statements.
892

894
895                Table 5: Definitions of five mathematical reasoning types in Lu et al. (2024).
896             Capability       Description
897                              It covers the fundamental operations such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, di-
           Arithmetic Reasoning
898                              vision, and understanding of number properties. It may also include the ability to
                  (80.8%)
899                              interpret numerical data in different forms.
900                              It focuses on data interpretation and analysis, including measures (mean, median,
           Statistical Reasoning
                                 mode), dispersion metrics (standard deviation, range), probability concepts, regres-
901               (77.9%)
                                 sion, correlation, and data inferences. It also identifies trends, outliers, and patterns.
902                              It encompasses understanding variables, equations, and the manipulation of expres-
           Algebraic Reasoning
903                              sions with polynomials and exponents. It also covers solving simple to complex equa-
                   (5.3%)
904                              tions, and grasping functions, their properties, and graphical depictions.
                                 It involves intuitive understanding of daily numerical concepts, including understand-
905       Numeric Commonsense
                                 ing time differences, numerical judgment, and estimates. It covers temporal reasoning,
906               (10.8%)
                                 spatial numeric assessments, and practical uses like budgeting and time reading.
907                              It focuses on critical thinking and deduction from provided information, including
            Logical Reasoning
908                              pattern recognition, sequence understanding, predictions, and statement evaluation.
                  (23,6%)
                                 Key components include premises, conclusions, and the use of abstract reasoning.

911
      A.2    F INANCIAL AND M ATHEMATICAL C APABILITY A NNOTATION
912
913   For five financial capabilities, we ask three human annotators to label each question in our dataset
914   with 1-to-2 capability. A question will be labelled with one capability if at least two annotators
915   choose this capability to label it. Specifically, questions that focus on the comprehension of finan-
916   cial terms and mathematical formulas are labeled as requiring terminology understanding. Questions
917   necessitating the model’s reasoning over time-series data, concepts, and mathematical formulas are
      categorized under temporal reasoning. When a question centers on predicting future trends, it is


                                                                17
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


918
      marked as requiring future forecasting. For questions that involve analyzing potential future sce-
919   narios to aid in decision-making, the label scenario planning is used. Lastly, questions that involve
920   creating structured representations of a company’s financial performance using financial statements
921   and informed assumptions are identified as needing model building.
922
      For mathematical capabilities summarized in Lu et al. (2024), we leverage gpt-4o-mini for anno-
923
      tation. Specifically, we use the prompt template in Appendix G.2.4 to annotate each calculation
924
      problem in our dataset.
925

940      Answer: False

946      match. Which one of the following swaps can help the bank offset risk?
947      A. Pay fixed and receive floating
948      B. Pay floating and receive fixed
949      C. Pay variable and receive fixed
950      Answer: A

      A.3.2    E XAMPLES OF T EMPORAL R EASONING
955
956      Example 1 of Temporal Reasoning in Financial Calculation task
957
958      You own 1,000 shares of stock in Avondale Corporation. You will receive a $1.50 per share
959
         dividend in one year. In two years, Avondale will pay a liquidating dividend of $45 per share.
         The required return on Avondale stock is 15 percent. What would be the equal dividend per
960
         share in each of the next two years to have the same present value as the current share price?
961
         (Unit: dollar)
962      Answer: 21.73
963

         Example 2 of Temporal Reasoning in Financial Calculation task
967
         The price of a European call that expires in six months and has a strike price of $30 is $2.
968
         The underlying stock price is $29, and a dividend of $0.50 is expected in two months and
969
         again in five months. Interest rates (all maturities) are 10%. If the stock price is above $30
970      in six months, what is the present value of the profit? (Unit: dollar)
971      Answer: 0.49

       A.3.3   E XAMPLES OF F UTURE F ORECASTING
973
974       Example 1 of Future Forecasting in Multi-choice Question task
975
976       Both Keynes’ and Friedman’s theories of the demand for money discuss the impact of in-
977       terest rates on money demand. According to Keynes model, which one of the following
978       outcomes happens when interest rates rise?
979       A. Demand for money decreases
980       B. Demand for money increases
981
          C. Demand for money stays unchanged
          Answer: A
982

985
986       Interest rates tend to change in response to the increase or decrease of aggregate output
987       during economic booms and recessions. Which one of the following actions might banks
988       take when output rises during a boom?
989       A. Freeze the level of their excess reserves
990       B. Reduce the level of their excess reserves
991
          C. Increase the level of their excess reserves
          Answer: B

          Example 1 of Scenario Planning in Multi-choice Question task
997
998       A trader sells a strangle by selling a call option with a strike price of $50 for $3 and selling a
999       put option with a strike price of $40 for $4. Within which one of the following price ranges
1000      of the underlying asset does the trader make a profit?
1001      A. Between $33 and $57
1002      B. Between $30 and $50
1003      C. Between $40 and $60
1004      Answer: A
1005
1006      Example 2 of Scenario Planning in Financial Calculation task
1007
1008      On May 8, 2013, an investor owns 100 Google shares. The share price is about $871 and
1009      a December put option with a strike price of $820 costs $37.50. The investor is comparing
1010      two alternatives to limit downside risk. The first involves buying one December put option
1011      contract with a strike price of $820. The second involves instructing a broker to sell the 100
1012      shares as soon as Google’s price reaches $820. How much will the investor pay to buy one
1013      December put option contract with a strike price of $820? (Unit: dollar)
1014
          Answer: 3750

1018
          Example 1 of Numerical Modelling in Financial Calculation task
1019
1020      Bedrock Gravel Corp.’s 2007 income statement shows the following information: sales =
1021      $162,000; costs = $93,000; other expenses = $5,100; depreciation expense = $8,400; interest
1022      expense = $16,500; taxes = $14,820; dividends = $9,400. Additionally, the firm issued
1023      $7,350 in new equity during 2007 and redeemed $6,400 in outstanding long-term debt. What
1024      is the 2007 operating cash flow? (Unit: dollar)
1025      Answer: 49080



                                                         19
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1026
           Example 2 of Numerical Modelling in Financial Calculation task
1027
1028       Winnebagel Corp. currently sells 30,000 motor homes per year at $45,000 each, and 12,000
1029       luxury motor coaches per year at $85,000 each. The company wants to introduce a new
1030       portable camper to fill out its product line; it hopes to sell 19,000 of these campers per year
1031       at $12,000 each. An independent consultant has determined that if Winnebagel introduces
1032       the new campers, it should boost the sales of its existing motor homes by 4,500 units per
1033       year, and reduce the sales of its motor coaches by 900 units per year. What is the annual
1034       sales figure due solely to the new portable camper product line? (Unit: dollar)
1035       Answer: 228000000

1038   A.4     R ELATED W ORKS OF V ISUAL - CONTEXT Q UESTIONS
1039
       Visual-context examples in XF IN B ENCH are closely related to multi-modal benchmarks that in-
1040
       volve chart understanding and reasoning, as shown in Table 6. Most related multi-modal bench-
1041   marks focus on descriptive question of charts that evaluates model’s perception ability. For example,
1042   multi-discipline multi-modal benchmarks, i.e,, MMMU (Yue et al., 2024), MMLU-Pro (Wang et al.,
1043   2024a) and MathVista (Lu et al., 2024), create descriptive questions around the visual information
1044   of charts, such as locating the number of a bar and finding the trend of a line. Although they em-
1045   phasize domain-specific knowledge for tackling their tasks, they stop at reading the technical terms
1046   that appear in the charts in finance domain. Moreover, there are many chart benchmarks that heavily
1047   rely on datasets from finance domain, including ChartQA (Masry et al., 2022), MMC (Liu et al.,
1048   2024) and CharXiv (Wang et al., 2024b). They focus on both chart understanding and reasoning,
1049
       while their reasoning tasks focus on multiple-step reasoning over the visual information, instead of
       domain-specific reasoning.
1050
1051   Visual-context questions in our XF IN B ENCH, however, require not only reasoning over visual in-
1052   formation of chart, but also interpreting the financial implications of data presented in the chart. For
1053   example, the chart in Figure 9 (a) evaluates model’s ability to find out the trend of exchange rate over
1054   time and then link it with the effect of exchange rate on the good price across two countries. The
1055
       former step focus on reasoning over visual information like previous works do, while the latter one
       requiring complex financial reasoning. Despite the small size of our visual-context questions, our
1056
       work is the first to explore the model’s potential of applying finance-domain knowledge to complex
1057
       chart reasoning. Examples of our visual-context questions are displayed in A.5.
1058
1059
1060   Table 6: Comparison of visual-context questions in XF IN B ENCH with existing multi-modal
1061   datasets. “# Image” refers to number of image; “# Ques.” refers to number of question; “NA”
1062   indicates not reported in the main body of the paper. For tasks, “MCQ” and “OQ” stand for multiple-
1063   choice question and open question, respectively.
1064

          Example 2 of finance term and definition

1190      Term: Total credit cost curve of optimal credit policy
1191      The trade-off between granting credit and not granting credit isn’t hard to identify, but it
1192      is difficult to quantify precisely. As a result, · · · The sum of the carrying costs and the
1193      opportunity costs of a particular credit policy is called the total **credit cost curve**. We
1194      have drawn such a curve. There is a point where the total credit cost is minimized. This
1195      point corresponds to the optimal amount of credit or, equivalently, the optimal investment in
1196      receivables.\n\n If the firm extends more credit than this minimum, the additional net cash
1197      flow from new customers will not cover the carrying costs of the investment in receivables.
1198
          If the level of receivables is below this amount, then the firm is forgosing valuable profit
          opportunities.\n\n In general, the costs and benefits from extending credit will depend on
1199
          characteristics of particular firms and industries. All other things being equal, for example,
1200
          it is likely that firms with (1) excess capacity, (2) low variable operating costs, and (3) repeat
1201      customers will extend credit more liberally than other firms. See if you can explain why
1202      each of these characteristics contributes to a more liberal credit policy.
1203
1204
1205
1206
1207
1208      Example 3 of finance term and definition
1209
1210      Term: Open market operations for control of Monetary Base
1211      The Federal Reserve exercises control over the monetary base through its purchases or
1212      sale of government securities in the open market, called **open market operations**, and
1213      through its extension of discount loans to banks. A purchase of bonds by the Fed is called
1214
          an **open market purchase**, and a sale of bonds by the Fed is called an **open market
          sale**.
1215
1216
1217
1218
1219
1220      Example 4 of finance term and definition
1221
1222      Term: Exchange-rate targeting
1223      Targeting the exchange rate is a monetary policy strategy with a long history. It can take
1224      the form of fixing the value of domestic currency to a commodity such as gold, the key
1225      feature of the gold standard described earlier in the chapter. More recently, fixed exchange
1226      rate regimes have involved fixing the value of the domestic currency to that of a large, low-
1227
          inflation country like the United States (the anchor country). Another alternative is to adopt
          a crawing target or peg, in which a currency is allowed to depreciate at a steady rate so that
1228
          the inflation rate in the pegging country can be higher than that of the anchor country.
1229
1230
1231
1232
1233
1234
          Example 5 of finance term and definition
1235
1236      Term: American call option
1237      Black suggests an approximate procedure for taking account of early exercise in call options.
1238      This involves calculating, as described earlier in this section, the prices of European options
1239      that mature at times T and tn , and then setting the American price equal to the greater of the
1240      two.15 This is an approximation because it in effect assumes the option holder has to decide
1241      at time zero whether the option will be exercised at time T or tn .



                                                         23
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

1248      causes the contract to have zero value. · · · The relationship between the price of this bond

       B     D ETAILED DATA C ONSTRUCTION
1297

       B.1     S OURCE DATA
1299
1300   The details of textbooks are displayed in Table 7. During data collection, annotators are instructed
1301   to adhere to copyright and license regulations, avoiding data from sites prohibiting copy and redis-
1302   tribution.
1303
1304
                                    Table 7: Details of textbooks as source data.
1305
1306                               Textbook                            Authors        Version # Chapters
1307                  Fundamentals of Corporate Finance            Stephen A. Ross       8       22
1308                 Options, Futures and Other Derivatives          John C. Hull        9       32
1309         The Economics of Money Banking and Financial Markets Frederic S. Mishkin    9       25

1311
       B.2     QA TASK AND AUTOMATIC A NNOTATION
1312
1313   We leverage GPT-4o to process after-class questions under a generate-then-verify framework (Zhang
1314   et al., 2024). Few-shot prompt templates for generate-then-verify framework are in §G.
1315
       For the generation stage, examples in the prompt template illustrate the rules of transforming open-
1316
       ended questions into those with clear final answers. For statement judging task, rules of creating
1317   false statements are: 1) antonym substitution, such as small → big; 2) object position interchange,
1318   such as “A is red and B is blue“ → “B is red and A is blue“; 3) adjective modification, such as “it
1319   is possible“ → “it is impossible“, etc. For multi-choice question answering task, we follow STARC
1320   (Berzak et al., 2020) rules to design two misleading choices that are mutually exclusive to but share
1321   the similar wording and length with the correct choice. For financial calculation task, calculation
1322   questions usually have a series of sub-questions that share the same solution in the gold answer but
1323   have different final answers. In this case, GPT-4o simply split the question into independent ques-
1324   tions with clear final answers. Furthermore, to ensure that the generated question contain necessary
1325
       information to get its final answer, we ask GPT-4o to extract the context in the after-class question
       first, and then extract the question and its final answer (see examples in prompt templates).
1326
1327   For the verification stage, rules for discarding unqualified questions are illustrated in the prompt
1328   templates in §G.
1329
1330   B.3     K NOWLEDGE BANK C ONSTRUCTION AND A NNOTATION
1331
1332   We collect finance terms from the subject index at the end of each textbook, and manually extract
1333
       their definitions from the chapter’s content. Specifically, for each term, we locate its corresponding
       pages indicated in the subject index, and collect the paragraphs related to this term. There are two
1334
       common cases during this process: (1) the term’s name is the title of a subsection, so its related
1335
       paragraphs are the main content of this subsection; (2) the term’s definition in the corresponding
1336   page is within a highlighted box, so we only collect the information within the box. Mathematical
1337   expressions and tabular information are also collected if any, while visual context of terms is not
1338   saved in our dataset. When retrieving relevant terms of a question, we concatenate the names of
1339   terms with their definitions for representing each term in the abstract space. It is worth noting that
1340   some terms may share the same pages, indicating that they share the same definition. Examples of
1341   term and definition are shown in A.6.
1342   To bridge questions and finance terms, three annotators are asked to identify 1-to-3 relevant finance
1343   terms from the knowledge bank to each question in XF IN B ENCH. For each question, annotators
1344   search for the relevant terms from those in the same textbook and chapter with this question. If
1345   the term is included, they verify its context and details for relevance. A finance term would only
1346   be annotated to the question when at least two annotators agree on the high relevance. Finally, a
1347   question has 1.3 finance term on average.
1348
1349



                                                         25
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

       C     M ORE DATASET A NALYSIS
1351
1352
1353
       C.1    H UMAN Q UALITY VALIDATION
1354
       We conduct a comprehensive validation protocol to ensure the high quality of the annotated data. For
1355
       each annotated question, we assign our three evaluators to validate whether: 1) the question contains
1356   complete information in the original question to get the final answer; 2) the final answer is correct
1357   given the original answer; 3) the associated knowledge terms are helpful for answering the question.
1358   We ask the evaluators to rate all examples in the test and validation sets of XF IN B ENCH on a scale
1359   of 1 to 5 individually. During this process, human evaluators are accessible to the corresponding
1360   after-class questions with gold answers and the knowledge bank. The result is illustrated in Table 8,
1361   indicating the high quality of our dataset.
1362
1363

       D     M ORE E XPERIMENT S ETUP
1459
1460
       D.1   E VALUATION ON B IZ B ENCH AND K NOWLEDGE FMATH
1461
1462   For BizBench (Koncel-Kedziorski et al., 2024), we randomly sample 500 examples from its test set.
1463   The reason why we select BizBench is that it covers most of previous finance dataset like TAT-QA
1464   and FinQA, and includes quantity extraction task that requires extracting numbers from contextual
1465   materials and conducting simple numerical reasoning. Additionally, we do not include SEC-NUM
1466   task of BizBench in our experiment due to its incomplete representation of questions.
1467   For KnowledgeFMATH (Zhao et al., 2024), we use its validation set with 200 examples and ground
1468   truths released. The reason why we select KnowledgeFMATH is that it first introduces more com-
1469   plex numerical-reasoning questions than quantity extraction task in finance domain. While our
1470   XF IN B ENCH is more complex and challenging for both MLLM and text-only LLM, it is still worth
1471   evaluating our baselines on KnowledgeFMATH for more comprehensive study.

1502   D.3   H UMAN P ERFORMANCE
1503
1504   We conducted a study to evaluate human performance in XF IN B ENCH. We randomly sampled 1,000
1505   questions from test set of XF IN B ENCH, with 400 of statement judging task, 170 of multi-choice
1506
       question answering task, and 430 of financial calculation task. Each question was then assigned to
       three human experts, all of whom have finance master degrees and have studied the courses covering
1507
       three textbooks in our source data. None of them is involved in the dataset construction work. The
1508
       human evaluation is conducted in a close-book setting, and allows standard calculators (not the
1509   financial ones). For each question in statement judging and multi-choice question answering tasks,
1510   they must complete each question within five minutes, while in financial calculation, the limit is ten
1511   minutes due to more reasoning process required in mathematical reasoning.

1620
       F     M ORE E RROR A NALYSIS
1621
1622
       F.1   H UMAN L ABELING G UIDELINE
1623
1624   For errors in financial calculation task, we sampled 400 responses of o1 and assign them to three
1625   annotators. Our annotators are asked to determine 1) whether the reasoning path of o1’s response
1626   coherets with the gold answer of corresponding correct answer; 2) whether there is rounding error in
1627   the intermediate calculating steps, i.e., rounding error; and 3) whether the formula in o1’s response
1628   is different from the formulas in the relevant finance terms, i.e., formula misuse. During this process,
1629
       annotators are provided with the gold answer of the corresponding after-class questions, which in-
       clude the correct reasoning path. The result of each dimension is decided by at least two annotator’s
1630
       agreement.
1631
1632   For errors in visual-context multi-choice question answering task, we sampled 100 responses of
1633   GPT-4o and assign them to three annotators. Our annotators are asked to determine the explanation
1634   in gpt-4o’s response is totally correct, partially correct, or wrong (Lu et al., 2024). For responses
1635
       with partially correct and wrong explanation, we further ask annotators to decide 1) if the response
       presents correct reasoning path with consistency and correct interpretation of visual context; 2) if
1636
       the response shows the model has difficulty identifying the positions and intersections of curves, i.e.,
1637
       blindness; and 3) if the response misuses financial knowledge that leads to the error in the following
1638   reasoning steps, i.e., knowledge misuse. The result of each dimension is decided by at least two
1639   annotator’s agreement.
1640
1641
       For errors in knowledge augmentation method, we sampled 100 responses of gpt-4o that give wrong
       final answers, and assign them to three annotators. Our annotators are asked to determine 1) whether
1642
       the first wrong reasoning step is triggered by the information in the augmented knowledge (reason-
1643
       ing error if no); 2) whether the augmented knowledge proposes direct solution or evidence to answer
1644   the corresponding question (over thinking if yes); and 3) whether the wrong reasoning path is led
1645   by following every detail in the augmented knowledge (over reliance if yes). During this process,
1646   annotators are provided with the gold answer of the corresponding after-class questions, which in-
1647   clude the correct reasoning path. The result of each dimension is decided by at least two annotator’s
1648   agreement.

1728
          Example of reasoning error in GPT-4o’s response in Oracle setting. Red sentences are re-
1729
          lated to the wrong reasoning step.
1730
1731      Question (Financial Calculation task; Numerical Reasoning capability):
1732      The Huff Co. has just gone public. Under a firm commitment agreement, Huff received
1733      $15.05 for each of the 5 million shares sold. The initial offering price was $16 per share,
1734      and the stock rose to $19.50 per share in the first few minutes of trading. Huff paid $800,000
1735      in direct legal and other costs and $250,000 in indirect costs. What were the total indirect
1736      costs incurred by the company? (Unit: dollar)
1737
1738
          Term and Definition:
          Indirect expenses,Flotation costs. Indirect expenses are not reported on the prospectus and
1739
          include the costs of management time spent working on the new issue.
1740
1741      Model Response:
1742      To determine the total indirect costs incurred by Huff Co., we only need to consider the
1743      provided information about the indirect expenses. The question states that Huff Co. incurred
1744      $250,000 in indirect costs. Since no other indirect costs are mentioned, these will be the
1745      total indirect costs. Therefore, my answer is [250000].
1746
1747      Correct Answer:
1748      17750000
1749
          Analysis:
1750
          The wrong reasoning path is not triggered by the information in the augmented knowledge.
1751
1752
1753      Example of over reliance error in GPT-4o’s response in Oracle setting. Red sentences are
1754      related to the wrong reasoning step while teal sentences enhance or correct the model’s
1755      reasoning process.
1756
1757      Question (Financial Calculation task; Scenario Planning capability):
1758      A stock price is currently $40. At the end of one month, it will be either $42 or $38.
1759      The risk-free interest rate is 8% per annum with continuous compounding. What is the
1760
          value of a one-month European call option with a strike price of $39? (Unit: dollar, decimal)
1761
          Term and Definition:
1762
          One-step,Binomial tree. A no-arbitrage framework for pricing options on a stock is
1763      presented, where the stock price is denoted as S0 and the current option price as f . ......
1764      Rearranging provides: f = S0 ∆(1 − ue−rT ) + fu e−rT . ...... This can be simplified to:
                      −rT
1765                      )+fd (ue−rT −1)                                        rT
          f = fu (1−de u−d                . We also introduce p defined as: p = eu−d−d
                                                                                       .
1766
1767      Model Response:
1768      To determine the value of a one-month European call option with a strike price of $39,
1769      we will use the provided binomial tree model framework along with the relevant equa-
1770      tions. Step-by-Step Solution ...... Determine the value of p using provided Equation:
                rT
1771      p = eu−d −d
                      = 1.0067−0.95
                         1.05−0.95 =
                                       0.0567
                                         0.1  = 0.567. Calculate the value of the call option using
1772                                                −rT
                                                          )+fd (ue−rT −1)
          another provided Equation: f = fu (1−de         u−d             = 1.70595
1773
1774      Correct Answer:
1775      1.69
1776
1777      Analysis:
1778      The last step calculation that leads to the wrong final answer is not necessarily so complex.
1779      The model could simply solve this problem via planning in different scenarios. The
1780      expected payoff E is given by: E = p · 3 + (1 − p) · 0 = 1.701. The value of the call option
1781      C is the discounted expected payoff: C = E · e−rT = 1.701 · 0.9934 ≈ 1.69.

1782
          Example of over thinking error in GPT-4o’s response in Oracle setting. Red sentences are
1783
          related to the wrong reasoning step while teal sentences enhance or correct the model’s
1784      reasoning process.
1785
1786      Question (Statement Judging task; Future Forecasting capability):
1787      Statement: Consider the operating procedures of the Fed in response to movements in
1788      output. If the Fed has interest-rate targets, a rise in output that raises interest rates might
1789      cause the Fed to sell bonds and bid down their price.
1790
1791      Term and Definition:
1792
          Traditional Interest-Rate Channels,Transmission Mechanisms of Monetary Policy.
          The traditional view of the monetary transmission mechanism illustrates how expan-
1793
          sionary monetary policy impacts the economy through a schematic representation:
1794
          ⇒ it ↓⇒ l ↑⇒ Y ↑.An expansionary monetary policy results in a decrease in real interest
1795      rates (it , ↓), which lowers the cost of capital, thus increasing investment spending (l ↑) and
1796      boosting aggregate demand, leading to a rise in output (Y ↑). Under interest-rate target, the
1797      government could hold back the changes on interest rate via open market operations. For
1798      example, selling bonds would cut down the interest rate due to the increasing money supply.
1799
1800      Model Response:
1801      Let’s break down the given statement and consider ......3. Fed’s Response with Interest-Rate
1802      Targeting: If the Fed observes a rise in interest rates due to increased output but desires
1803
          to maintain its interest-rate target, it would engage in open market operations to influence
          these rates. 4.Selling Bonds to Lower Prices and Raise Rates: The Fed sells bonds in
1804
          open market operations to reduce the money supply. Selling bonds bids down their price,
1805
          which inversely raises the yield or interest rates. This action can counter rising rates if the
1806      objective is to maintain higher interest rates to dampen potential inflationary pressures.
1807      Putting it together...... Therefore, my answer is true.
1808
1809      Correct Answer:
1810      No
1811
1812      Analysis:
1813      After the third reasoning step, the correct answer could be proposed. However, the model
1814
          thinks one step further to consider other influencing factors in the long run, and finally
          give the wrong final answer. Note that the third reasoning step highly follows the part of
1815
          augmented knowledge highlighted in blue.

1839   G.1     S ENSITIVITY A NALYSIS
1840
1841   We conduct sensitivity analysis on prompt templates for evaluation on XF IN B ENCH. ProSA (Zhuo
1842   et al., 2024) showcases four different styles of constructing prompts, i.e., simple input (SI), emo-
1843   tional support (ES), role player (RP) and output requirement (OR). We further include two common
1844
       prompting strategies, i.e., chain-of-though (CoT) and direct answering (DA). Hence, we design four
       types of prompt templates for conducting our sensitivity analysis, as shown in Table 18. Note that
1845
       output requirement is indispensable in our tasks for automatic evaluating the model’s final answers.
1846
1847
1848
1849                 Table 18: Four prompt templates for sensitivity analysis during evaluation.
1850         Capability  Task
1851                     You are a financial expert. You are supposed to answer the given question.\n Question:
1852                     {after-class question}\n Please answer the above question and output your
           CoT & RP & OR
1853                     final answer starting with ’Therefore, my answer is’ at the end, where you store you
1854                     final answer into ’[]’.\n Let’s think step by step.\n
1855                     You are a financial expert. You are supposed to answer the given question.\n Question:
                         {after-class question}\n Please answer the above question and output your
1856       DA & RP & OR
                         final answer starting with ’Therefore, my answer is’ at the end, where you store you
1857                     final answer into ’[]’.\n
1858                     Question: {after-class question}\n Please answer the above question and
1859         CoT & OR    output your final answer starting with ’Therefore, my answer is’ at the end, where you
1860                     store you final answer into ’[]’.\n Let’s think step by step.\n
1861                     Question: {after-class question}\n Please answer the above question and
1862         DA & OR     output your final answer starting with ’Therefore, my answer is’ at the end, where you
                         store you final answer into ’[]’.\n
1863
1864
1865
1866   We randomly sample 500 examples from the test set of XF IN B ENCH and use them to evaluate four
1867   models on each of prompt templates in Table 18. Experiment results in Table 19 show that the
1868   prompt template involving chain-of-though, role player and output requirement consistently leads
1869   to outstanding performance of most models across three tasks, and brings out the best performance
1870
       of most models with slight margins. Additionally, Chain-of-thought strategy outperforms direct
       answering strategy under most cases since our financial tasks require intensive mathematical and
1871
       logical reasoning (Sprague et al., 2024). Despite the slight differences of performance across four
1872
       prompt templates, the rankings of four models hardly change in three tasks and the overall scores.
1873
1874
1875

       cial calculation tasks, are shown in G.2.1, G.2.2, and G.2.3, respectively. Additionally, we use the

       prompt in G.2.4 to annotate the calculation problems in our dataset with mathematical reasoning
1898   abilities summarized in Lu et al. (2024).

          Prompt template for generating true statements in statement judging task.
1912
          Please rewrite a question-answer pair into one or more statement(s) which is/are true.
1913
          Specifically,
1914      1. The statement(s) should be generated from the original question-answer pair and must be
1915      true given the content of the question-answer pair.
1916      2. None of the following expressions is allowed in the statement: (1) unclear pronoun;
1917      (2) in/given/according to the chapter/figure/table; (3) conjunctions of causality like since,
1918      because and so on.
1919      3. You should extract the context of the original question. The context usually introduces
1920      the background of the generated statement(s). Note that: (1) the context must NOT be
1921      question; (2) there should NOT be duplicated or contradictory information between the
1922
          context and the statement.
          4. You are allowed to generate two or more statements from one question-answer pair.
1923
          Under this case, the statements should be independent of each other, with as little overlap as
1924
          possible.

          Example 1 in prompt template for generating true statements in statement judging task.
1945
1946      Original Question: Suppose that a bond portfolio with a duration of 12 years is hedged
1947      using a futures contract in which the underlying asset has a duration of four years. What is
1948      likely to be the impact on the hedge of the fact that the 12-year rate is less volatile than the
1949      four-year rate?
1950
1951      Original Answer: Duration-based hedging procedures assume parallel shifts in the
1952      yield curve. Since the 12-year rate tends to move by less than the 4-year rate, the portfolio
1953      manager may find that he or she is over-hedged.
1954
          Context: Suppose that a bond portfolio with a duration of 12 years is hedged using a
1955
          futures contract in which the underlying asset has a duration of four years.
1956
1957      Statement: Considering duration-based hedging procedures assume parallel shifts in
1958      the yield curve, the portfolio manager may find that he or she is over-hedged.
1959
1960      Answer: True

1982
1983      Original Answer: The delta of a stock option measures the sensitivity of the option
1984      price to the price of the stock when small changes are considered. Specifically, it is the ratio
1985      of the change in the price of the stock option to the change in the price of the underlying
1986      stock.
1987

1989
          Statement: The delta of a stock option measures the sensitivity of the option price to
1990
          the price of the stock when small changes are considered.

1995      stock option to the change in the price of the underlying stock.

          Example 3 in prompt template for generating true statements in statement judging task.
1999
2000      Original Question: The treasurer of a corporation is trying to choose between options and
2001      forward contracts to hedge the corporation’s foreign exchange risk. Discuss the advantages
2002      and disadvantages of each.
2003
2004      Original Answer: Forward contracts lock in the exchange rate that will apply to a
2005      particular transaction in the future. Options provide insurance that the exchange rate will
2006      not be worse than some level. The advantage of a forward contract is that uncertainty
2007      is eliminated as far as possible. The disadvantage is that the outcome with hedging
2008
          can be significantly worse than the outcome with no hedging. This disadvantage is not
          as marked with options. However, unlike forward contracts, options involve an up-front cost.
2009
2010
          Context: The treasurer of a corporation is trying to choose between options and for-
2011      ward contracts to hedge the corporation’s foreign exchange risk.
2012
2013      Statement: Options provide insurance that the exchange rate will not be worse than
2014      some level.
2015      Answer: True
2016
2017      Statement: When using forward contracts for hedging, the outcome can be worse
2018      than the outcome with no hedging.
2019
          Answer: True
2020
          Statement: Using options to hedge the foreign exchange risk involve an up-front
2021
          cost.
2022      Answer: True
